### Question 1: I am writing to request your review of my course tracking in preparation for graduation. I am pursuing a double major in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. I have attached the spreadsheet tracking my courses for your review. I would appreciate it if you could take a look and provide any necessary feedback: Humility, Global Humanities and Social Change, Linear Algebra, Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society, Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age, Discrete Math, Computer Science II: Data Structures, Programming for Data Science and Visualization, Design and Systems Thinking, Scientific Inquiry, Computer Organization, Calculus, Intelligent Robot Studio: From Theory to Practice, How to become a writer: lessons from Vietnamese and non-Vietnamese writers, Introduction to Political Science, The Global Vietnam War II, Deep Learning for Artificial Intelligence, Advanced Deep Learning, Probability, Computer Science I: Introduction to Programming, Algorithms and Theory of Computing, Database System, Advanced Programming Techniques, Introduction to Artificial Intelligence, Machine Learning for Data Science, Multivariable Calculus, Mathematical Statistics, General Work Program (GWP) 1, [Independent Research Project] Labeled FreSCo- Mining Frequent Patterns in labeled Simplicial Complexes, Object-Oriented Analysis and Design, Statistical Learning, Operating Systems, Abstract Algebra, Differential Equations, Stochastic Calculus, Natural Language Processing, Advanced Linear Algebra.  
### Answer: 

The course tracking spreadsheet you've provided shows a comprehensive and well-rounded selection of courses that align with a double major in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. Here's a breakdown and some suggestions for ensuring you meet all degree requirements:

Core Curriculum:
- You've completed all the core courses required by the university: Global Humanities, Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society, Design and Systems Thinking, and the QUEST courses (Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age, Scientific Inquiry, Introduction to Statistics, and Programming for Data Science and Visualization).
- Ensure that you have met the specific requirements for the Class of 2027 and earlier, which include five core courses (as opposed to the current requirement of four).

Exploratory Requirements:
- You've taken a variety of courses that cover a broad spectrum of subjects, which is excellent for exploring interests and gaining a well-rounded education.

Major Requirements:
- Computer Science Major:
  - You've covered the foundational Computer Science courses such as Computer Science I & II, Computer Organization, Database Systems, Operating Systems, and several advanced courses like Machine Learning for Data Science, Deep Learning for AI, and Natural Language Processing.
  - Ensure you've met the specific course requirements for the Computer Science major, which may include additional electives or specific advanced courses.
- Applied Mathematics Major:
  - You've taken a strong set of mathematics courses including Linear Algebra, Calculus, Probability, Mathematical Statistics, Abstract Algebra, and Differential Equations.
  - Make sure you've completed all the required Pure Mathematics courses (e.g., Real Analysis, Advanced Linear Algebra) as per the degree requirements.
  - Consider taking additional electives in Applied Mathematics to deepen your understanding and expertise in the field.

Elective and Experiential Learning:
- You've included several electives that complement your majors, such as Intelligent Robot Studio and The Global Vietnam War II, which can provide interdisciplinary perspectives.
- Look for opportunities for experiential learning such as internships, research projects (like your Independent Research Project), or participation in research seminars.

Capstone Experience:
- You've mentioned an Independent Research Project, which is a great capstone experience for your double major. Ensure this project is well-documented and meets the requirements for assessment.
- Consider taking the CS 401/402 Capstone I and II if they are available and required for your degree.

Overall, your course selection demonstrates a strong commitment to your academic goals. However, it's crucial to review the specific degree requirements for both majors, as they may have additional or specific course requirements that you need to fulfill. Also, ensure that you're not double-counting courses for both majors and that you're meeting the credit hour requirements for graduation.

Lastly, since you're on a path to graduation, it's important to stay in close communication with your academic advisors to ensure that you're on track and to get any updates on degree requirements that may have changed since your initial course planning.

Please review the university's official course catalog and consult with your advisors to confirm the current requirements and to address any potential gaps in your course plan. Your proactive approach to course tracking is commendable, and with a little fine-tuning, you should be well on your way to a successful graduation.

### Question 2: Can you differentiate a cross-listed course and elective applied course in the case of a double major?  
### Answer: 

Cross-listed courses are those that are shared between two different majors or a major and a minor, and they can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements up to 8 credits (two courses). Elective applied courses, on the other hand, are courses from other majors that emphasize the applications of Mathematics. These courses are not necessarily shared between majors or minors but are chosen by the student to supplement their major studies with practical applications of mathematical concepts.

In the case of a double major, cross-listed courses can be particularly useful because they allow a student to count the same course toward both majors, up to a limit of 8 credits. This can help the student streamline their course load and potentially reduce the number of courses they need to take.

Elective applied courses, meanwhile, are chosen to fulfill the requirement of taking at least 2 applied courses (at least one at the 200 level). These courses are from other disciplines (like Economics, Engineering, Computer Science) and are intended to provide the student with a broader understanding of how mathematical concepts are applied in various fields.

To differentiate:

- Cross-listed courses are shared between majors or a major and a minor and can be double-counted up to 8 credits.
- Elective applied courses are chosen from other majors to provide practical applications of mathematical concepts and cannot be double-counted for two different requirements.

In summary, cross-listed courses are about efficiency and streamlining requirements between two areas of study, while elective applied courses are about broadening the student's exposure to practical applications of mathematics in various fields.

and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. ### Question 3: I would like to ask for the capstone withdrawal policy. What will be the impact when I choose to drop the capstone before the Fall 2025 term starts? Will there be any penalties associated with withdrawing, such as a 'W' notation on my transcript?  
### Answer: 

According to the provided policy, the withdrawal timeline is crucial for understanding the impact of withdrawing from a course, including a capstone course. For the Fall 2025 term, the first withdrawal period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4. During this period, students can withdraw from a course without any consequence, meaning no 'W' notation will appear on their transcript. However, if you choose to withdraw from the capstone course during this time, there will be no impact on your GPA or transcript, as no grade is recorded.

If you decide to withdraw from the capstone course after the end of Week 4 (i.e., during the second withdrawal period, which starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 8), attempted credits and a "W" grade will be recorded on your transcript. This "W" grade does not affect your GPA.

It is important to note that withdrawing from a capstone course, especially if it is a requirement for your degree, could have significant implications for your academic progress and graduation timeline. Therefore, it is advisable to consult with your academic/major advisor before making a decision to withdraw.

Regarding penalties, there are no explicit penalties mentioned for withdrawing within the specified periods. However, withdrawing from a capstone course could potentially delay your graduation or affect your ability to meet program requirements, which might have indirect consequences.

In summary, if you withdraw from the capstone course before the end of Week 4 of the Fall 2025 term, there will be no 'W' notation on your transcript and no impact on your GPA. However, withdrawing after Week 4 will result in a 'W' grade being recorded, which does not affect your GPA but could have other academic implications. Always consult with your academic advisor before making such decisions.

### Question 4: Questions related to course history, in which one course can have multiple IDs? Like Advanced Deep Learning course. How would it be handled among different cohorts?  
### Answer: 

Course history would involve tracking changes and updates to a course over time, including different versions, iterations, or cohorts. When a course like "Advanced Deep Learning" is offered to multiple cohorts, each cohort might have its own unique identifier (ID) to distinguish it from others.

For instance:

- **Cohort 1 ID:** ADL-C1
- **Cohort 2 ID:** ADL-C2
- **Cohort 3 ID:** ADL-C3

Each cohort ID would allow for the tracking of specific course materials, assignments, deadlines, and student progress separately for each group. 

Course history records would include:

- **Course Title:** Advanced Deep Learning
- **Course Versions:** V1, V2, etc.
- **Cohort IDs:** ADL-C1, ADL-C2, ADL-C3, etc.
- **Dates:** Start and end dates for each cohort
- **Instructors:** Names of instructors for each cohort
- **Content Updates:** Notes on changes made to the course content over time

The system would need to be designed to handle these multiple IDs and versions to ensure that each cohort's experience is tracked accurately and separately from others. This might involve a database structure that includes a primary course ID and a secondary cohort ID, allowing for detailed querying and reporting on both the overarching course and the specific instances of it.

The references provided (COURSE ID: repeated five times) are irrelevant to answering the question as they do not provide any context or information about how course history or cohort management is handled. The answer is based on general principles of course management and tracking in educational settings.

### Question 5: I have taken Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis in Fall 2023. Am I eligible to take Financial Economics in Spring 2024?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you are eligible to take Financial Economics in Spring 2024. According to the given context, students must complete at least one of Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis before taking Advanced level courses. Since you have completed both Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis in Fall 2023, you meet the prerequisite requirement to enroll in an Advanced level course like Financial Economics in the subsequent semester, which in this case is Spring 2024. The course descriptions and requirements clearly state that Advanced level courses can be taken after completing at least one of these two courses. Therefore, you are ready to proceed with Financial Economics in the next term.

### Question 6: If I have waived the course Computer Science I, but I want to declare Computer Science major, do I have to take Computer Science I?  
### Answer: 

No, you do not have to take Computer Science I if you have waived it and still want to declare Computer Science major. The requirement to declare the major is having completed at least two Computer Science courses, with MATH 202 - Discrete Math also counting towards this requirement. The foundational course of Introduction to Programming (CS 101) is typically the first course that students take to explore different areas of computer science, but if you have successfully waived this course, you can proceed directly to other required courses such as Data Structures & Algorithms (CS 201) and Discrete Math (MATH 202). The curriculum structure allows for flexibility and progression through foundational, intermediate, and advanced courses to build a strong foundation in computer science.

### Question 7: If I have taken the following courses: Mathematical Statistics, Abstract Algebra, Linear Algebra, Multivariable Calculus, Calculus, Differential Equation, Stochastic Calculus, Statistical Learning, Discrete Mathematics, Probability, am I eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics? What about majoring in Applied Mathematics?  
### Answer: 

To determine eligibility for a minor in Applied Mathematics, we first need to examine the requirements typically set by a university for such a minor. Generally, a minor in Applied Mathematics might require a certain number of courses, often including a mix of foundational, intermediate, and possibly advanced courses in areas such as Linear Algebra, Calculus (both single and multivariable), Statistics, and possibly more specialized topics depending on the institution.

Given your course list:

- **Mathematical Statistics**: This course covers foundational statistical concepts and methods, which is relevant to Applied Mathematics.
- **Abstract Algebra**: While this is more of a pure mathematics course, it provides a strong foundation in mathematical reasoning and proof, which can be beneficial.
- **Linear Algebra**: Essential for Applied Mathematics, this course is typically a requirement.
- **Multivariable Calculus**: Crucial for understanding higher-dimensional calculus applications, which are common in Applied Mathematics.
- **Calculus**: Typically a prerequisite for more advanced calculus courses.
- **Differential Equation**: A core topic in Applied Mathematics, often required for a minor.
- **Stochastic Calculus**: Advanced topic relevant to Applied Statistics and Financial Mathematics, which is part of Applied Mathematics.
- **Statistical Learning**: Relevant to Data Science and Applied Statistics, a key area of Applied Mathematics.
- **Discrete Mathematics**: Important for understanding mathematical structures used in computer science and engineering, which are applied fields.
- **Probability**: Fundamental for understanding statistical methods and stochastic processes.

Based on the typical requirements for a minor in Applied Mathematics, you have covered a broad spectrum of foundational and intermediate-level courses that are essential for the minor. However, specific requirements can vary, so it's crucial to check the exact requirements at your institution.

**For a minor** in Applied Mathematics, you likely meet the foundational and intermediate course requirements, assuming your institution values a broad exposure to the field rather than a strict specialization. However, you should consult with an academic advisor to confirm eligibility, as some programs might have additional prerequisites or specific course requirements.

**Majoring in Applied Mathematics** is a different matter. To major in Applied Mathematics, you typically need to demonstrate a deeper understanding and specialization in the field. This usually involves advanced courses in specific areas of Applied Mathematics, such as:

- **Advanced Statistics**
- **Statistical Learning**
- **Stochastic Calculus**
- **Numerical Analysis**
- **Optimization**
- **Partial Differential Equations**

Given your course list, you have a strong foundation and have taken several advanced courses relevant to Applied Mathematics. However, to major in Applied Mathematics, you would likely need to take additional advanced courses to specialize further in one or more areas of Applied Mathematics, such as those listed above. Again, it's crucial to consult with an academic advisor to understand the specific requirements and to plan your course of study accordingly.

In summary, you are well-positioned to pursue a minor in Applied Mathematics based on your current course work. For a major, while you have a solid foundation, you would need to take more advanced courses specific to Applied Mathematics to meet the major requirements. Always consult with an academic advisor for the most accurate and personalized guidance.

### Question 8: I have completed these 100-level courses: Introduction to Art History and Theory, Introduction to Film History and Theory. Am I eligible to start a Capstone in my major Arts and Media Studies?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you are eligible to start a Capstone in your major Arts and Media Studies. To be eligible for the Capstone, you must meet the following criteria: a major GPA of 3.6 and have completed all 100-level major requirements, which includes having completed Introduction to Visual Studies and two Foundational courses. Since you have completed Introduction to Art History and Theory and Introduction to Film History and Theory, you have fulfilled the requirement for two of the Foundational courses. Therefore, you have met the eligibility criteria for starting the Capstone. However, you still need to have completed the Introduction to Visual Studies course to formally declare the major and be fully eligible for the Capstone. Once you have completed this prerequisite, you can apply for Capstone I with your 3.6 major GPA, provided you have no outstanding Incompletes in your major courses. The successful application for Capstone I is also required for enrollment.

### Question 9: Is it possible that I write a book as my History Capstone?  
### Answer: 

The History Capstone project at Fulbright University allows for a great deal of flexibility in format, and while the handbook specifically mentions traditional thesis writing, alternative formats such as a short documentary film, podcast, historical exhibition, or an interactive digital narrative are also acceptable. Writing a book as your Capstone project is certainly within the realm of possibility, provided it meets the general requirements and expectations set forth by the History faculty.

Here's how you might approach it:

1. **Consult with Faculty**: Reach out to potential advisors and discuss your idea of writing a book as your Capstone project. They can provide guidance on whether this format is feasible and how it might be structured to meet the Capstone's objectives.

2. **Adapt to Guidelines**: Ensure that your book project aligns with the Capstone's objectives, which include demonstrating historical reasoning, effective communication, and the production of original work using under-utilized sources. Your book should intervene in the current state of knowledge in a particular field of history.

3. **Scaffolded Support**: Since the Capstone involves scaffolded peer-review and support, you would need to design your project in close consultation with history faculty, ensuring you receive the necessary guidance throughout the process.

4. **Written Component**: Even if your project is a book, it should have a substantial written component that can be evaluated. This might mean focusing on a specific section or chapter of the book that can stand alone as a piece of historical analysis.

5. **Approval and Evaluation**: The History faculty will establish guidelines for your book project format if it's not a traditional thesis. They will confer and establish criteria for evaluation, including the defense process with two readers.

6. **Honors Eligibility**: Your book project could be eligible for honors evaluation, which would require it to meet high standards of originality, research, and writing.

In summary, writing a book as your History Capstone is possible, but it requires careful planning, faculty approval, and adherence to the Capstone's objectives and evaluation criteria. It's important to discuss your idea with faculty early in the process to ensure it aligns with the program's expectations and to receive the necessary support and guidance throughout the project.

The Capstone project is a year-long undertaking with less structure than a traditional course. It is certainly not the easiest way to complete your degree, and it may not be the most logical option for you, depending on your interests and your post-graduation plans. You should not consider it to be the default pathway to finishing your degree. One of the best reasons to undertake a Capstone project is if you enjoy doing historical research (which you will have sampled in Hands-On History and the research seminars you have completed) and want an opportunity to carry out a long-term research project. For such students, the Capstone year provides time and guidance that is unparalleled by any course or other opportunity at the undergraduate level. However, if you wish to graduate with distinction—an honorable goal— but you don’t particularly enjoy doing independent research, you may want to skip the Capstone and focus on GPA honors instead! ### Question 10: Given I have finished these subjects: Advanced Deep Learning, Machine Learning, Deep Learning, Database. Are they sufficient for the Advanced Courses requirement of the computer science major?  
### Answer: 

The Advanced Courses requirement typically involves taking more specialized and advanced courses beyond the foundational courses. From the list provided, you have taken courses that are advanced in nature, such as Advanced Deep Learning, Machine Learning, Deep Learning, and Database Systems. These courses cover important and cutting-edge topics within computer science.

However, it's important to note that the specific requirements for the Advanced Courses can vary depending on the institution and the particular curriculum. Some programs might require a certain number of advanced courses from a specific list, or they might require a certain breadth across different areas of computer science.

Given the courses you've mentioned, you have a strong foundation in machine learning and deep learning, which are highly relevant and advanced topics. However, to ensure you meet the Advanced Courses requirement, you should:

1. Review the specific requirements of your computer science major's curriculum. Check the course catalog or consult with an academic advisor to understand exactly what is expected.

2. Consider any additional advanced courses that might be required or recommended. For instance, courses in areas like Natural Language Processing (CS312), Computer Vision (ENG301), or specialized topics in Bioinformatics (CS3xx) could be beneficial.

3. Ensure that you have met any prerequisites for any additional advanced courses you might need to take.

4. Keep in mind that while the courses you've taken are advanced, the requirement might also include a certain level of depth or breadth across different subfields of computer science.

In summary, the courses you've taken are certainly advanced and relevant, but you should verify the specific requirements with your academic institution to ensure full compliance with the Advanced Courses requirement for your computer science major.

### Question 11: I have a question if I take a gap year, will my financial aid be canceled? If yes, can I still re-apply for the financial aid program when I get back to school?  
### Answer: 

Yes, taking a gap year may result in the cancellation of your financial aid. It is important to meet with the Student Financial Services team as soon as possible to discuss your situation and explore alternative solutions. They will attempt to determine the circumstances and may request a timely response from you. If your financial aid is canceled, you will need to reapply for financial aid when you return to school. The specific process and requirements may vary, so it is crucial to contact the financial aid office for guidance on how to proceed. Keep in mind that you will also need to follow the re-admission process to return to your studies at Fulbright, as outlined in the provided context. This includes meeting with your academic advisor to discuss a plan to complete your degree and ensuring that all necessary steps are taken before departing from the university.

### Question 12: I am aware that Fulbright has to organize online classes instead of on-campus ones. Therefore, will there be any change in the tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

No, there will be no change in the tuition fee due to the format of the course (online, on-campus, blended). The tuition fee remains the same regardless of the course format. However, students should be aware that course withdrawals may affect their financial aid eligibility if resulting in fewer than the minimum credits for full-time enrollment. Additionally, students who withdraw from courses may need to refer to the "Fees and Charges Guide" for refund options, if applicable. The withdrawal process requires supporting documentation, authorization from the course instructor(s), academic/major advisor, and the Registrar’s Office, and it follows the "Fees & Charges Guide" for tuition credit in the case of approved exceptional circumstances. It's important to note that no reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Students should also be aware of the impact on their waitlist status and any missed assignments if they are added to a course from the waitlist.

### Question 13: If I major in Economics, is it sufficient that I take Scholar Development to fulfill Experiential Learning requirements?  
### Answer: 

Yes, Scholar Development can be an appropriate course to fulfill the Experiential Learning requirements for an Economics major at Fulbright University. The Experiential Learning Program (ELP) at Fulbright University exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. The university requires all full-time, undergraduate, degree-seeking students to complete at least 4 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement.

The Scholar Development course, as part of the ELP, is designed to provide students with opportunities to engage in reflective learning experiences that contribute to their personal and professional development. It typically involves activities that promote self-awareness, goal setting, and the development of skills relevant to academic and career success.

Given that Scholar Development is an approved course under the ELP, it can be an effective way for Economics majors to meet their Experiential Learning requirements. However, it's always a good idea to discuss your course selection with your academic advisor to ensure it aligns with your academic goals and fits within the requirements of your major.

In summary, Scholar Development can be a relevant and valuable course for Economics majors to fulfill their Experiential Learning requirements, provided it is approved by the university and fits within the student's overall academic plan.

### Question 14: Is it sufficient for me to complete 4 credits in EL if I major in Engineering?  
### Answer: 

Yes, it is sufficient for you to complete 4 credits in Experiential Learning (EL) if you are majoring in Engineering. The degree requirements for a bachelor's degree in Engineering specify that you must complete Experiential Learning, which includes at least 4 credits of Scholar Development (which must be approved by the Chair of Engineering). The total Experiential Learning requirement ranges from 4 to 12 credits, but the minimum requirement is 4 credits. Therefore, meeting the minimum requirement of 4 credits in Experiential Learning is sufficient to fulfill this part of the Engineering degree requirements. However, it's important to note that the Scholar Development component must be approved by the Chair of Engineering to ensure it aligns with the program's expectations and goals.

### Question 15: Can I take all four engineering foundation courses and only two intermediate engineering courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, according to the degree requirements, you can take all four engineering foundation courses. However, the degree requirements specify that you must take at least 9 engineering major courses (36 credits), of which at least 3 courses must be at the 300-level or Advanced level (12 credits). This means you can take up to 6 engineering major courses at the foundation or intermediate levels, but you must ensure that at least 3 of those courses are at the 300-level or Advanced level. Therefore, you could take all four foundation courses and then take two intermediate courses, but you would still need to take at least one 300-level or Advanced level course to meet the requirement of 12 credits at that level. The degree requirements do not explicitly limit the number of intermediate courses you can take, but they do require a specific number of advanced-level courses. Therefore, while you can take a combination of foundation and intermediate courses, you must ensure that you meet the advanced-level course requirement.

### Question 16: If I have completed the course Intro to AI, will CS207 - Object-Oriented Analysis and Design and CS211 – Operating Systems suffice to fill the rest two intermediate courses?  
### Answer: 

CS207 - Object-Oriented Analysis and Design is a course that focuses on strengthening understanding of object-oriented concepts and building skills in developing software systems. It covers topics like class, object, information hiding, encapsulation, inheritance, interface, polymorphism, design patterns, multi-threading, database connection, unit testing, and Unified Modeling Language (UML). Students work in teams to design software using UML models and implement the design via building Android applications using Java.

CS211 – Operating Systems, on the other hand, teaches students about the fundamentals of operating systems, including process management, memory management, file systems, and I/O. It provides a solid understanding of how operating systems manage resources and provide services to applications.

Both courses are important for a comprehensive computer science education, but they do not directly fulfill the prerequisites for all other AI-related courses listed. For instance, CS307 Machine Learning, which is a direct follow-up to AI concepts, requires CS201 (Intro to Programming) as a prerequisite, not CS207 or CS211.

While CS207 and CS211 are valuable courses that contribute to a well-rounded computer science education, they do not specifically cover the learning methods and problem-solving techniques that are central to AI, as introduced in the Intro to AI course. Therefore, they may not be sufficient to fill the gap for AI-related intermediate courses without additional relevant coursework.

To continue with AI-related studies, you would likely need to take additional courses that build on the foundational AI principles introduced in the Intro to AI course. For example, CS307 Machine Learning specifically builds on AI concepts and would be a logical next step. However, without specific prerequisites listed for other AI-related courses, it's best to consult the course catalog or a faculty advisor to ensure you're taking the correct sequence of courses to meet your educational goals in AI.

In summary, while CS207 and CS211 are valuable courses, they may not be sufficient to fill the gap for AI-related intermediate courses without additional relevant coursework. It's important to check the prerequisites for the specific courses you're interested in and consult with an academic advisor to ensure you're on the right track for your AI studies.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: CS_major_handbook.pdf, Page: 19]
(AI) is a research ﬁeld that studies how to realize the intelligent human behaviors on a computer. The ultimate goal of AI is to make a computer that can learn, plan, and solve problems autonomously. In this course, student will learn the foundational principles and practice implementing some of these applications including representation, problem solving, and learning methods of artiﬁcial intelligence. Accordingly, students should be able to develop intelligent systems by assembling solutions to concrete computational problems; understand the role of knowledge representation, problem solving, and learning in intelligent-system engineering; and appreciate the role of problem solving, vision, and language in understanding human intelligence from a computational perspective. ### Question 17: Does completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfy the core theory requirement, or is an additional course needed?  
### Answer: 

Completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfies the core theory requirement for the Economics major. According to the outlined requirements, students must complete two Foundation courses, which include Principles of Economics 1 and 2 (covering microeconomics and macroeconomics respectively). These courses provide the essential overview of economic theories and are fundamental to understanding the functioning of the economy. Therefore, taking both Principles of Economics 1 and 2 ensures that students have met the core theory requirement without needing an additional course.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Major Descriptions and Flowcharts_OfficialVersion.pdf, Page: 31]
Courses: Principles of Economics 1 and 2 (100-level) These two courses introduce microeconomics and macroeconomics, providing an overview of economic theories and analytical techniques applied to current issues. By studying both microeconomics (the analysis of choices made by people, firms, and governments) and macroeconomics (the analysis of the economy as a whole) students will develop their economic intuition as they better understand the functioning of the world. Methodology Courses: Economic Methods (100-level) This course introduces basic concepts and methods of statistical analysis and fundamental rules of mathematics used in economic analysis. Students will be exposed to a wide range of economic problems and the methods to solve them. By completing this course, students will be qualified to take upper-level courses in the Economics major. ### Question 18: If I want to major in Engineering, can I choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II, for instance Introduction to data visualization?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II if you want to major in Engineering. For instance, Introduction to Data Visualization (CS103) is a relevant course that aligns with data science and visualization skills, which are important in many engineering disciplines. This course specifically aims to equip students with the skills necessary to analyze and visualize data, which can be applied in various engineering contexts such as data-driven decision making, system modeling, and performance analysis.

The course description for CS103: Programming for Data Science and Visualization states that it aims to provide students with a set of skills to analyze the nature of data across different domains. It demonstrates data visualization by understanding, questioning, and problematizing how data are generated, analyzed, and used. This course will enable students to apply concepts and skills to visualize data, interpret findings, and examine the impacts of data-driven decisions.

Given that engineering often involves dealing with complex data sets and requires the ability to interpret and visualize data effectively, CS103 can be a valuable addition to an engineering student's curriculum. It complements the foundational programming skills taught in CS101 (Introduction to Programming) and CS104 (Introduction to Computer Science), providing a more specialized focus on data analysis and visualization.

Moreover, the course structure and prerequisites (none, with CS104 being recommended) make it accessible to students who have completed introductory programming courses. This means that even if you start with CS101 or CS104, you can then move on to CS103 to gain specialized skills in data science and visualization.

In summary, while Computer Science I and Computer Science II are core courses for a Computer Science major, engineering students have the flexibility to choose courses that best fit their interests and career goals, such as CS103: Programming for Data Science and Visualization. This course can enhance an engineering student's ability to work with data, make informed decisions, and innovate in their field.

### Question 19: Given I took Discrete Mathematics in Spring 2022 and earned B, then the same course in Spring 2024 and earned C-, which grades will be kept?  
### Answer: 

The higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript.

### Question 20: Can I register 300-level math courses as exploratory courses E4?  
### Answer: 

No, the Exploratory requirements specify that students must take courses at the 100-level or 200-level to fulfill these requirements. The context states that students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses) from each of the three categories (E1, E2, and STEM), and these courses must be at the 100-level or 200-level. The 300-level math courses are advanced courses intended to help students shape their study path into specific applied fields, and they are not eligible to be used for the Exploratory requirements. Therefore, you cannot register 300-level math courses as exploratory courses E4.

### Question 21: Can I register for more than 20 credits in a semester?  
### Answer: 

No, according to the provided context, students at Fulbright University Vietnam are limited to registering for a maximum of 20 credit hours per term. To register for more than this limit, students must complete the "Maximum Course Load Request" form, obtain the approval of their Academic Advisor, and submit the form to the Office of the Registrar before the end of the online registration period. The typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term, and students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term to make standard progress toward completing their degree. Students wishing to take fewer than 12 or more than 16 credit hours in one term must submit a specific form for approval. Therefore, registering for more than 20 credits in a semester is not permitted without prior approval through the proper channels.

All students for whom English is not their first language must take an English Assessment Test required by NECHE Accreditation. 13.8  Double Counting In satisfying degree requirements, some courses can be double-counted. For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. ### Question 22: What is the maximum number of times I can retake a course to improve my grade, and how does it affect my transcript?  
### Answer: 

A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time. When repeating a course, a higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits.

### Question 23: For a student doing the literature capstone, if a student includes charts or tables, do they count toward the word limit?  
### Answer: 

In the provided guidelines, charts or tables, along with images with captions, are explicitly mentioned as not counting towards the word limit. For example, in the guidelines provided:

2.1. Word count regulations: "The written thesis is documentation of the capstone project, in the form of a research paper. There is no word count limit, but the thesis is usually 25-40 pages on A4 size. This page count includes full bibliography and images with captions."

2. Capstone by Thesis Guidelines: 2.1. Word Count Regulations: "The final submission should be 10,000-15,000 words, including endnotes or footnotes, but not including full bibliography and images with captions."

3. Guidelines for the Capstone by Thesis: 3.1. Word Count: "Generally, you should aim for a word count between 6,000 and 10,000 words. This will probably amount to less than 60 pages when double spaced, unless you include a large number of images, tables, or other non-textual items."

Based on these guidelines, charts or tables, along with images and captions, do not count towards the word limit. The word count is determined solely by the textual content (including endnotes or footnotes), and the inclusion of visual aids does not affect the overall word count requirement. Therefore, a student can include charts or tables without worrying about them affecting the word limit.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Integrated Sciences_Capstone Handbook_14Jan2025.pdf, Page: 13]
2. Capstones by Thesis guidelines: 2.1. Word count regulations The written thesis is documentation of the capstone project, in the form of a research paper. There is no word count limit, but the thesis is usually 25-40 pages on A4 size. This page count includes full bibliography and images with captions. 2.2. Style and formatting The thesis should be typed with font size 12, suggested fonts are Calibri or Arial. The following parts should be included in order: • Title page • Committee signature page • Copyright page (do NOT number or add anything to this page, only change the template* to your name) • Acknowledgement page • Abstract • Table of contents • Introduction • Methods • Results • Discussion • Conclusions • References *Templates for the first 3 pages can be found on the Capstone II Canvas page. Following these general formatting guidelines, the details of the thesis and presentation should be worked out between the student and advisors. 2.3. ### Question 24: If I failed Capstone I in the Fall 2024, can I redo it in Spring 2025?  
### Answer: 

Yes, if you fail Capstone I in the Fall 2024 semester, you would need to address the feedback from your primary advisor and make any necessary adjustments to your project. You would then be able to re-enroll in Capstone I for the Spring 2025 semester. However, it's important to note that you must pass Capstone I before you can proceed to Capstone II. The primary advisor would assess the deliverables from Capstone I and decide if the student passes. Only when a grade of "pass" is submitted by the primary advisor can the student be registered for Capstone II. If you do not pass Capstone I, you should promptly plan your IS courses to fulfill the major requirements, ensuring you manage your academic progress effectively. The Capstone Handbook also advises good time management and completing as many graduate requirements as possible prior to the capstone to avoid such setbacks.

However, if you wish to graduate with distinction—an honorable goal— but you don’t particularly enjoy doing independent research, you may want to skip the Capstone and focus on GPA honors instead! Students work with a primary advisor and, to a lesser extent, a secondary advisor. The format and length of projects is flexible, within certain limits. ### Question 25: If I do Literature capstone in Decolonial studies, can it be approved?  
### Answer: 

The Literature capstone project can be approved if there is faculty expertise in the proposed field, such as Decolonial studies. According to the given contexts, current full-time faculty in Literature hold expertise in various fields, including postcolonial studies, which is closely related to Decolonial studies. Therefore, if a student proposes a capstone project in Decolonial studies and can find a faculty member with relevant expertise to serve as an advisor, the project can be approved. However, it is crucial for the student to consult with prospective advisor(s) early in their junior year to determine the suitability and feasibility of the proposed project, as stated in the guidelines. This ensures that the project aligns with the faculty's expertise and the program's requirements.

Through support from existing faculty, students propose, undertake, and write up a research or creative writing project over the course of two consecutive semesters, labelled as Capstone I and Capstone II. Students are also expected to think very carefully about an appropriate scope of research and production plan for the proposed capstone. The project may also draw from academic coursework, relevant extracurricular activities or practical experience to support the research and realization of the project. ### Question 26: I am a Co24 majoring in Computer Science and taking the Database course. Does it count as major courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, CS304: Database Systems counts as a major course for Computer Science majors. According to the course catalogue, CS304: Database Systems is a prerequisite for the Computer Science major and is listed under the major courses. It is a comprehensive examination of the design and implementation of relational database management systems (DBMS), covering topics such as logical organization of databases, E_R design, normalization, SQL for data description and retrieval, concurrency and security issues, and includes a major project building web interfaces to databases using PHP and MySQL. Additionally, it introduces students to NoSQL solutions. Given these details, it is clear that CS304 is an integral part of the Computer Science curriculum and thus counts as a major course.

### Question 27: Can Discrete Mathematics be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors?  
### Answer: 

No, Discrete Mathematics (MATH 202) can only be counted once towards the requirements of either the Computer Science major or the Applied Mathematics major, not both. According to the given context, MATH 202 is considered a prerequisite for the Computer Science major and can be counted towards the Computer Science requirements. For the Applied Mathematics major, students must take at least one Pure Mathematics course, and while MATH 202 is listed under Sample Applied Mathematics Courses, it cannot be double-counted for both majors. Therefore, Discrete Mathematics cannot be counted for both majors simultaneously.

### Question 28: What steps should I take to ensure a smooth transfer of my exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

To ensure a smooth transfer of your exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright, follow these steps:

1. **Gather Required Information**: Compile all necessary details about the Bard College courses you wish to transfer, including course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments.

2. **Complete the Application**: Fill out the Application for Credit Transfer form provided by Fulbright. This form requires your personal information, Bard College course details, and your academic advisor's information.

3. **Attach Official Transcript**: Ensure that an official transcript with a seal from Bard College is sent directly to the Office of the Registrar at Fulbright University Vietnam. The email address for submission is registrar@fulbright.edu.vn, or you can use the mailing address specified in the Transfer Credit Policy.

4. **Review Transfer Credit Policy**: Before submitting your request, read the Transfer Credit Policy V1.0 published in the One-Stop portal to understand the criteria and process.

5. **Declaration**: Make sure to sign the declaration section of the application form, affirming that the information provided is complete and correct.

6. **Submit the Application**: Submit your completed application form along with the required course information and official transcript to the Office of the Registrar.

7. **Follow-Up**: After submission, keep in touch with your academic advisor and the Office of the Registrar to track the progress of your credit transfer application.

8. **Understand the Limitations**: Be aware that a maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree.

By following these steps, you will provide all the necessary information and adhere to the proper procedures for a smooth transfer of your credits. Remember that Fulbright will determine the transferability of your credits after you have enrolled, so ensure all documentation is accurate and submitted promptly.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
courses delivered by Fulbright. When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. Information may include but not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. To apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption, students should submit an Application for Credit Transfer, along with the required information on the course(s), to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified. Credit Transfer from A-level, Advanced Placement (AP), International Baccalaureate (IB), and Cambridge International Exam (CIE) courses will be determined according to the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy (in development). For more information, please refer to the Transfer Credit policy_Final_V3.0 published in the One Stop portal.

### Question 29: Are 100-level and 200-level courses that are not explicitly categorized as E1, E2, E3, or E4 also excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement?  
### Answer: 

Yes, according to the provided context, only courses explicitly chosen from the categories E1 (Arts and Humanities), E2 (Social Sciences), and STEM can be used to fulfill the Exploratory requirements. Courses at the 100-level or 200-level that are not categorized into these specific exploratory categories are excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement. The text explicitly states that students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses) from each of the three categories (E1, E2, and STEM) to meet the requirement. Therefore, any 100-level or 200-level courses that do not fall into these specified categories cannot be used to satisfy the Exploratory requirement.

### Question 30: I am a Co25 student pursuing a Computer Science major, can I choose to follow either the old flowchart or the new one?  
### Answer: 

The question does not specify which "flowchart" you are referring to. However, based on the provided contexts, there are two distinct plans outlined for Computer Science majors: a 15-course plan and a 56-credit plan. The 15-course plan seems to be an older structure, while the 56-credit plan appears to be a newer, more detailed curriculum.

The 15-course plan is structured as follows:
- Year 1: Introduction to CS, Computer Science I, Discrete Math
- Year 2: Computer Science II, Computer Organization, two electives
- Year 3: Algorithms & Theory of Computing, two intermediate CS courses, two electives
- Year 4: Three advanced CS courses, Capstone I & II or two elective intermediate/advanced courses

The 56-credit plan is structured as follows:
- Foundational Courses: Introduction to Programming (4 credits), Data Structures & Algorithms (4 credits), Computer Organization (4 credits), Discrete Math (4 credits)
- Intermediate Courses: Three intermediate courses (12 credits)
- Advanced Courses: Four advanced courses (16 credits)
- Capstone: Capstone I and Capstone II (8 credits)
- Electives: 8 additional credits

As a Co25 student (assuming Co25 refers to a cohort or class of 2025), you would typically follow the curriculum that is currently in effect for your year of study. If the 56-credit plan is the current curriculum for Computer Science majors at your institution, you would follow that plan.

If the 15-course plan is still in use or if there is an option to choose between plans, you would need to consult with your academic advisor to determine which plan is appropriate for you based on your academic goals, the requirements of your institution, and any transfer credits you may have.

In summary, without knowing which "flowchart" you are referring to, it is recommended to follow the current and most updated curriculum for your major. If there is flexibility to choose between plans, consult with your academic advisor to make an informed decision.

### Question 31: If I withdraw from a course after the 4th week of the semester, will I be deducted 4 credits of financial aid?  
### Answer: 

No, withdrawing from a course after the 4th week of the semester will not necessarily result in a deduction of 4 credits of financial aid. According to the provided policy, a student who withdraws from a course beyond the halfway point (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course) automatically receives an 'F' grade, which will affect the student’s GPA. However, the withdrawal itself does not guarantee a deduction of financial aid credits. The financial aid eligibility is affected if the withdrawal results in fewer than the minimum credits required for full-time enrollment, which is 12 credits per term. Therefore, the specific impact on financial aid would depend on the student's overall credit load after the withdrawal, not the number of credits from the specific course being dropped. Students should consult with the financial aid office to understand the exact impact on their financial aid package after a course withdrawal.

### Question 32: Why do I need to take 8 exploratory courses?  
### Answer: 

Exploratory requirements encourage students to step out of their comfort zone by exploring broad areas of study and discover more fully where their interests and passions lie. Students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) chosen from each of the three categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. No course can be taken to fulfill more than one category. At most, 8 exploratory credits can be taken as Pass/No Pass. Up to two 8 exploratory credits (2 courses) can be counted towards the student’s major requirements. If a student has more than one major, the 8-credit limit is independently applied to each major. Students should aim to complete the Exploratory requirements by the end of their second year.

### Question 33: Can I take 160 credits in 4 years given that I pay for the full tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright University Vietnam requires students to earn a minimum of 128 credit hours to graduate with an undergraduate degree. The typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term, which means a student can earn 64 credit hours over four years if they follow the standard course load. However, the maximum number of credit hours a student can transfer from another institution is 64. This means even if a student transfers the maximum allowable credits, they would still need to complete at least 64 credit hours at Fulbright to meet the graduation requirement.

To take 160 credits in four years, a student would need to register for more than the standard course load each term. The standard progress requires students to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. To exceed this, students must submit a Petition for Alternative Course Load or Maximum Course Load Request form to the Registrar's Office, which must be approved by their Academic/Major Advisor.

Given the tuition fee structure at Fulbright University Vietnam, the cost would be spread out over the four years, assuming the standard tuition fee applies regardless of the number of credits taken per term. However, taking more than the standard course load would mean a student would be paying for more courses and potentially more intensive study, which could impact their academic performance and overall experience.

In summary, while it is technically possible for a student to take 160 credits in four years by exceeding the standard course load, they would need to petition the university for permission to do so and ensure they have the academic support and time management skills to handle the increased workload. The tuition fee would be paid in accordance with the university's fee structure, which likely charges per credit hour, so the total cost would be higher than if the student took the standard course load.

of credit hours a student successfully completes with a grade of D or higher for letter grade options and a Pass for Pass/No Pass options. STUDENT ENROLLMENT: 3.1 Course Load and Credit Hours Fulbright University Vietnam uses the “credit hour” as the quantum for course workload in the undergraduate program. A credit hour typically represents 3 hours of work during each week of a 16-week term. For a 4- credit hour course, a student would, therefore, be expected to put in 12 hours of work per week, including class time, for a total of 192 hours of work during a term. There are earned credits and attempted credits. Earned credits refer to the number of credit hours a student successfully completes with a grade of D or higher for letter grade options and a Pass for Pass/No Pass options. Attempted credits are the credit hours students enroll in and are recorded in the transcript, regardless of whether they complete the courses or pass them. This includes all courses for which a student receives a grade, including withdrawals (W), incompletes (I), repeats (RP), and failures (F). See section 4 for more details on different types of grades. To graduate

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
their Fulbright undergraduate degree. This is referred to as Credit Transfer. In addition, a student who has credit for a course passed at another university that is evaluated to be equivalent to a course at Fulbright may be given an Exemption from being required to do that Fulbright course. A maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree. That is, to earn a Fulbright University Vietnam undergraduate degree, a student must earn at least 64 credit hours from courses delivered by Fulbright. When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. Information may include but not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. To apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption, students should submit an Application for Credit Transfer, along with the required information on the course(s), to the Office of the Registrar.

### Question 34: What are the requirements for mathematics courses to pursue Human-Centered Engineering major?  
### Answer: 

Students are required to take at least 1 Pure mathematics course. They will have to take at least 2 advanced courses in Mathematics, which can be in areas such as Applied Statistics, Engineering Mathematics, or Data Science. Additionally, they must take at least 2 applied courses from other majors that emphasize the applications of Mathematics, with at least one at the 200 level. Consultation with an academic advisor is required for course selection.

For example, Operations research can only count for either 1 Advanced course or 1 Elective course, but not both. Pure Mathematics courses: These courses provide the essential abstract concepts and structures that connect various branches of Mathematics and help students understand the adaptability of Mathematics to different types of problems. ### Question 35: How is the grade of a replaced course displayed on the transcript? Will it replace the old one or create a new entry in the transcript?  
### Answer: 

The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript.

### Question 36: What are the courses that can replace the capstone for a given major?  
### Answer: 

Students who do not do a capstone will need to fulfill 8 credits of coursework as specified in their major. Individual majors will determine eligibility requirements, and students should discuss their proposals with program coordinators and potential advisors to prepare their applications.

Students pursuing a double major must complete all requirements for each major. ### Question 37: Is it possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics? If yes, please provide a sample study plan.  
### Answer: 

It is possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering (HCE) and Applied Mathematics, but it requires careful planning and adherence to the university's curriculum requirements for both majors. Here's a sample study plan:

**Year 1:**
- Fall Semester:
  - Calculus I (Pure Mathematics)
  - Introduction to Engineering (HCE)
  - Freshman Seminar (General Education)
- Spring Semester:
  - Calculus II (Pure Mathematics)
  - Introduction to Computer Science (HCE)
  - Introduction to Statistics (Applied Mathematics)
  - General Education Course

**Year 2:**
- Fall Semester:
  - Linear Algebra (Pure Mathematics)
  - Introduction to Human-Centered Design (HCE)
  - Introduction to Quantitative Finance or Operations Research (Applied Mathematics)
- Spring Semester:
  - Multivariable Calculus (Pure Mathematics)
  - Engineering Ethics (HCE)
  - Advanced Statistics or Statistical Learning (Applied Mathematics)
  - General Education Course

**Year 3:**
- Fall Semester:
  - Differential Equations (Applied Mathematics)
  - Human-Computer Interaction (HCE)
  - Elective Applied Course (Economics, Engineering, Computer Science)
- Spring Semester:
  - Optimization (Applied Mathematics)
  - Capstone Design Project I (HCE)
  - Elective Applied Course (Economics, Engineering, Computer Science)

**Year 4:**
- Fall Semester:
  - Partial Differential Equations (Applied Mathematics)
  - Capstone Design Project II (HCE)
  - Research Seminar (Applied Mathematics)
- Spring Semester:
  - Advanced Topics in Applied Mathematics (e.g., Numerical Analysis)
  - Senior Thesis or Project (HCE)
  - Research Seminar (Applied Mathematics)

**Notes:**
- Students must ensure they meet the prerequisite requirements for each course.
- The Applied Mathematics major requires at least 2 advanced courses and 2 applied courses, which can be chosen from a variety of options to complement the HCE curriculum.
- The HCE major typically requires a capstone design project and a senior thesis or project, which can be interdisciplinary and incorporate mathematical modeling or data analysis.
- Students should consult with academic advisors in both departments to ensure they are meeting all degree requirements and to plan their electives effectively.
- It is crucial to double-check the specific course offerings and prerequisites at the university, as these may vary.

This plan assumes a standard four-year undergraduate program and does not account for any additional requirements such as language courses, internships, or other general education courses that might be necessary for graduation. Students should also be prepared to manage a heavy course load and should consider the workload implications of double majoring.

### Question 38: Are the 4-credit EL counted towards the credits covered by financial aid?  
### Answer: 

The Experiential Learning (EL) credits, as per the provided context, are specifically designed to be part of the graduation requirements and can count toward elective or major requirements, subject to advisor approval. However, the specific question about whether these 4-credit EL credits are counted towards the credits covered by financial aid is not directly addressed in the given information.

The context states that all full-time, undergraduate, degree-seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the Experiential Learning Program (ELP) as a graduation requirement. It does not specify whether these credits are automatically covered by financial aid or if they are subject to the same rules as other elective or major credits in terms of financial aid eligibility.

Generally, financial aid packages are determined based on the total number of credits a student is enrolled in during a semester, and the cost of attendance (COA) which includes tuition, fees, room and board, books, supplies, and other personal expenses. Whether the 4-credit EL courses are included in the calculation for financial aid would depend on the institution's policies and how they define the COA.

In summary, without specific institutional policies or guidelines provided in the context, it is not possible to definitively state whether the 4-credit EL courses are automatically covered by financial aid. Students should consult with the Financial Aid Office at Fulbright University Vietnam to understand how these credits are treated for financial aid purposes.

For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. ### Question 39: What are the exchange policies of Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright University Vietnam has several exchange policies in place to support students effectively. These policies include expectations for citizenship, attendance, re-admission to full-time status, and leaves of absence for personal and medical reasons. Students are expected to behave with respect towards the community, fully engage with their courses, and adhere to specific attendance policies set by instructors. For re-admission, students must reapply and follow the academic regulations in effect at the time of readmission. Leaves of absence require advance notice and approval, with specific forms to be completed, and students must plan for making up credits upon their return. Medical leaves are also addressed, allowing students to take a leave if their health significantly interferes with their ability to succeed at Fulbright. These policies are subject to regular updates as the university grows and evolves.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 5]

### Question 40: What actions are considered plagiarism? Are AI-generated works counted?  
### Answer: 

Plagiarism involves presenting text, images, or other content created by others or by generative AI as your own work, even at the sentence level. This includes using AI-generated content without proper attribution. AI-generated works are indeed counted as plagiarism if they are presented as original work without acknowledging their source or the role of AI in their creation. Fulbright University Vietnam's Code of Academic Integrity explicitly states that presenting AI-generated content as one's own is grounds for failure of the Capstone I and/or Capstone II. This rule underscores the importance of transparency and originality in academic work, ensuring that students maintain integrity and honesty in their projects. The comprehensive documentation required for projects involving generative AI serves to highlight the AI's role and the human contributions, thereby addressing the ethical and copyright concerns associated with AI usage. This documentation is crucial for maintaining academic integrity and for ensuring that the originality and human contributions to the project are properly recognized.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: VIETNAM STUDIES CAPSTONE GUIDELINES.pdf, Page: 6]
Clearly deﬁne the reasons for utilizing generative AI and the speciﬁc elements of the project it contributes to. • Tools and Platforms: Specify the AI tools, models, or platforms employed. • Process and Methodology: Outline how AI will be incorporated, detailing which aspects of the project will be generated, improved, or altered with the use of AI. • Ethical and Copyright Issues: Address the ethical and copyright concerns associated with using generative AI and how these will be addressed throughout the project. This comprehensive documentation promotes transparency, oﬀers insight into AI's role in the creative process, and highlights the originality and human contributions to the project. 1.8. Grading process All Capstones are graded by the primary and secondary advisor, using what is known as a double-marking process. 1.9. ### Question 41: Why is the graduation requirement 128 credits, but the major requirement is only 48 credits?  
### Answer: 

The graduation requirement of 128 credits encompasses the Core Curriculum (12 credits), Exploratory requirements (16 to 32 credits depending on the class year), major requirements (48 credits), electives (which can vary but must total at least 128 credits overall), and potentially Experiential Learning requirements (which are subject to school policy). The major requirements specifically are detailed to be 48 credits, which include foundational courses like Introduction to Programming, Data Structures & Algorithms, Computer Systems, and possibly a capstone project or additional advanced coursework. The total of 128 credits ensures a broad and comprehensive educational experience, allowing students to explore various subjects, meet specific major requirements, and have flexibility through electives to tailor their educational journey according to their interests and career goals. The breakdown ensures that students not only gain a deep understanding in their chosen major but also develop a well-rounded educational foundation. The major requirements alone (48 credits) are a significant portion of the overall credits, but they are complemented by other requirements to ensure a well-rounded education.

### Question 42: Explain the cross-listed mechanism between courses.  
### Answer: 

Cross-listed courses are courses that are offered by two or more departments. They share the same course number, title, and credit hours, but may have different course descriptions. Students can enroll in cross-listed courses through any department offering the course. This mechanism allows students to explore different disciplines related to the same topic, and it also helps departments share resources and expertise. The cross-listing process typically involves collaboration between the departments involved, including agreement on course content, objectives, and assessment methods. It is important to note that while cross-listed courses share many similarities, there may be slight differences in the way the course is taught or the specific learning outcomes emphasized by each department. Students should check the course descriptions provided by each department to ensure they meet their academic goals and requirements. The cross-listed mechanism is designed to enhance the educational experience by providing students with a broader perspective on a subject and by allowing departments to collaborate effectively. However, the specific implementation and management of cross-listed courses can vary between institutions, so it's always a good idea to consult with academic advisors or department representatives for the most accurate and up-to-date information.

### Question 44: How does the core quest course selection work to fulfill core credits?  
### Answer: 

The core QUEST course selection works by requiring students to complete one course from a core group consisting of four courses: Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age (4 credits), Scientific Inquiry (4 credits), Introduction to Statistics (4 credits), and Programming for Data Science and Visualization (4 credits). These courses are pre-assigned to students in their first year or the first semester of the second year. The selection of one of these courses is mandatory for all students to fulfill the core curriculum requirements, contributing 4 credits towards the total 128 credits needed for graduation. The specific course chosen can vary based on the student's interests and career aspirations, but all options are designed to provide a foundational understanding of quantitative and scientific thinking, which are essential for a comprehensive liberal arts education. The courses are structured to ensure that students develop essential skills in reasoning, inquiry, and data analysis, which are applicable across various disciplines.

### Question 45: Can I take a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement?  
### Answer: 

No, the major requirements must be fulfilled with courses at the appropriate level. The important note in the context states that until the university can offer a robust list of 200 and 300-Level Courses, students can satisfy the 48 Credits requirement with any combination of 200 and 300-Level Courses. However, it specifically asks students to be guided by the requirement to select courses at the appropriate level where possible. This means that while students can use 300-Level Courses to meet the total credit requirement, they should prioritize using 200-Level Courses to fulfill specific major requirements. Therefore, while a 300-Level Course can contribute to the overall major credit requirement, it cannot be used to fulfill a 200-Level major requirement.

### Question 46: If I take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright, but Fulbright doesn’t have that major code, yet the course is related to a major at Fulbright, can I count it toward that major?  
### Answer: 

No, if a course taken at another university does not have an equivalent major code at Fulbright University Vietnam, it cannot be directly counted toward a major at Fulbright. The university's policy states that all courses used to fulfill major or minor requirements must be taken for a letter grade and must have an equivalent course at Fulbright. Credit Transfer and Exemptions are determined based on the equivalence of the course to a course offered at Fulbright, and a maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree. Therefore, if the course does not have a direct equivalent, it cannot be counted toward the major requirements. Students should apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption by submitting an Application for Credit Transfer along with the required information to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will then coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine if credit or an exemption is justified. However, without an equivalent course at Fulbright, it is unlikely that the course will be accepted for major requirements.

A student who has earned credit at another school or university may apply to have that credit counted towards their Fulbright undergraduate degree. This is referred to as Credit Transfer. In addition, a student who has credit for a course passed at another university that is evaluated to be equivalent to a course at Fulbright may be given an Exemption from being required to do that Fulbright course. For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard
